Dynamic superluminal warp drives

What happens to the causal structure when we consider the creation of a superluminal warp drive starting from initially flat spacetime? For concreteness, we study a simple case in which we reach the final velocity v = v 0 at a finite time which we take to be t = 0. We modify the metric of Eq. (1.64) introducing a (1.89) with f (r) defined in Eq. (1.65) and ds 2 = −c 2 dt 2 + [dx − v(r, t)dt] 2 , (1.88) where v(r, t) = v 0 δ(t)f (r),δ(t) ≡ e t/τ if t < 0, 1 if t ≥ 0. (1.90) Again, we can change coordinates, keeping the center of the bubble at rest (r = 0). This can be obtained by defining dr ≡ dx − v 0 δ(t)dt. (1.91) This is an exact differential form and can be integrated to get r =    x − v 0 τ e t/τ − 1 if t < 0, x − v 0 t if t ≥ 0. (1.92) The metric becomes 93) and the apparent horizons associated with the t slicing are found by settingv = −c. In this case a solution does not exist for any value of t, so that the apparent horizons are created at infinity at some finite t H . We show this below. Let us write the equation for the apparent horizons in the following form: ds 2 = −c 2 dt 2 + [dr −v(r, t)dt] 2 , v(r, t) = v 0 δ(t) [f (r) − 1] ,(1.f (r) = 1 − c v 0 δ(t) .

(1.94)

The function f takes all the values between 0 and 1. In particular, f (r) → 0 for r → ±∞ and f (0) = 1. Besides, the right-hand side of Eq. (1.94) is a monotonic function of t, such that, for t → −∞, 1 − c/ (v 0 δ(t)) → −∞ and reaches the value 1 − c/v 0 > 0 for t ≥ 0. As a consequence, there exists a time t H < 0 so that, for t > t H , there are always two solutions of Eq. (1.94), corresponding to a black and a white horizon. These horizons are born at t = t H at spatial infinity and at t = 0 they settle at two fixed positions r 1 and r 2 . Keeping these points in mind, we are able to build the Penrose diagram for the dynamic warp drive ( Fig. 1.6). At early times the metric is approximately Minkowskian, because δ(t) → 0 for t → −∞. Therefore, the causal structure is initially Minkowskian. Then, it progressively changes till t = 0. At this time one has built a stationary warp drive, just as in the previous section. After this time, the Penrose diagram looks exactly equal to that in Fig. 1.5. The final Penrose diagram is just obtained by gluing together the two behaviors. Again, we are not drawing an analytically extended diagram but only the relevant patch for the analysis that follows in Chap. 2. Given that a timelike observer can reach H + C and H + R in a finite proper time, the geometry can be extended in the future, beyond these lines. This is actually a subtle point. In fact, H + C and H + R (which are linked to the formation of a white horizon) are on the boundary of the Cauchy development of I − . In this sense, they are Cauchy horizons given that initial data are assigned only on I − . Hence, as noticed in Sec. 1.2.6, a possible extension would not be unique. In any case such an extension will not be relevant for what follows, given that we shall limit ourselves to investigate the asymptotic behavior of the RSET associated with the onset of the superluminal warp drive.

Let us stress that there are other possible ways to create a dynamical warp drive. In Sec. 2.3, we shall choose a different interpolation between the Minkowski and the warp-drive metrics, in which the horizons appear both at finite time and at finite space positions.
