Hence, the threshold frequency ω max is fixed by the asymptotic value of w(x → −∞). In our velocity flows of Eq. (4.3), one has ω max = Λ f (D). (4.17) As discussed above, in backgrounds containing one sonic horizon, there are two or three ABM, depending on whether ω is larger or smaller than ω max . Thus,φ is can be expanded as [see also Eq. (4.15)] φ BH = ∞ 0 dω e −iωt φ u ωâ u ω + φ v ωâ v ω + θ(ω max − ω)(ϕ −ω ) * â † −ω +e +iωt (ϕ u ω ) * âu † ω + (ϕ v ω ) * âv † ω + θ(ω max − ω)φ −ωâ−ω . (4.18) As for homogeneous profiles, the modes φ u ω and φ v ω have positive norm and they are associated with the usual real roots of the dispersion relation (4.13) (see Fig. 4.1). They describe, respectively, propagating right-u and left-v moving waves with respect to the condensed atoms. On the contrary, (ϕ −ω ) * has negative norm. It is associated with the extra roots that exist in supersonic flows for ω < ω max , and describes phonons that are trapped inside the horizon (x < 0) in the supersonic region. These modes describe the negative frequency partners of the outgoing Hawking phonons represented by φ u ω andâ u ω . In our infinite condensates, the above modes become superpositions of plane waves both in the left and right asymptotic regions. We can thus construct without ambiguity the in-and out-mode bases. The in modes are such that each of them contains only one asymptotic branch with group velocity directed towards the horizon. The definition of out modes is analogous and based on the criterion that each out mode contains only one asymptotic branch with group velocity directed away from the horizon. For instance, the right-going negative norm ingoing mode (Fig. 4.2, left panel) is the wave (ϕ in −ω ) * , whose projection on the left-going mode φ v ω vanishes in the right asymptotic region and whose projection on the right-going mode φ u ω vanishes in the left asymptotic region. The right-going positive norm u out mode ( Fig. 4.2, right panel) is the wave φ u,out ω whose projections on the left-going modes φ v ω and ϕ * −ω vanish in the left asymptotic region.

It is clear from the above discussion and Eq. (4.15) that, for ω > ω max , there exist only two positive norm modes and, therefore, in-and out-mode bases are linearly related by a trivial (elastic ) 2 × 2 transformation φ u,in ω = T ω φ u,out ω + R ω φ v,out ω , φ v,in ω =R ω φ u,out ω +T ω φ v,out ω ,(4.19) with the condition imposed by unitarity |T ω | 2 + |R ω | 2 = 1, (4.20) R ωT * ω + T ωR * ω = 0. (4.21) Instead, when ω < ω max , the in and out bases contain three modes which mix with each other in a nontrivial way by a 3 × 3 Bogoliubov transformation [131] φ u,in ω = α ω φ u,out ω + β −ω ϕ out −ω * +Ã ω φ v,out ω , (ϕ in −ω ) * = β ω φ u,out ω + α −ω ϕ out −ω * +B ω φ v,out ω , φ v,in ω = A ω φ u,out ω + B −ω ϕ out −ω * + α v ω φ v,out ω ,(4.22) where we denote with The standard normalization of the modes yields relations such as (from the first equation) |α ω | 2 − |β −ω | 2 + |Ã ω | 2 = 1,(4.23) where the minus sign in front of the β coefficient comes from the normalization of the negative norm mode (ϕ −ω ) * . 2 Given a quantum state |Ξ , which is an eigenstate of the three initial number operatorŝ can be expressed in terms of initial ones n i,in ω ≡â i,in † ωâ i,in ω ,(4.n i,in ω ≡ Ξ|â i,in † ωâ i,in ω |Ξ ,(4.26) by using the coefficients of the scattering matrix (4.22): n u,out ω = n u,in ω + |A ω | 2 (n v,in ω − n u,in ω ) + |β ω | 2 (1 + n u,in ω + n in −ω ), n v,out ω = n v,in ω + |Ã ω | 2 (n u,in ω − n v,in ω ) + |B ω | 2 (1 + n v,in ω + n in −ω ), n out −ω = n u,in −ω + |β −ω | 2 (1 + n u,in ω + n u,in −ω ) + |B −ω | 2 (1 + n v,in ω + n in −ω ). (4.27)

The first term in every equation is the initial occupation number of the corresponding mode. In the first two equations, the second term is due to the elastic scattering between right-and left-going modes. The third term, which is due to the presence of negative norm modes, is the sum of spontaneous (represented by the 1 in the parenthesis) and stimulated emission involving the initial occupation number of the partner and the species itself. In the third equation the elastic scattering term is missing, but there are two spontaneous/stimulated emission terms related to the two pair-creation channels.

When the initial state is vacuum,the final occupation numbers are n u,out ω = |β ω | 2 , (4.28) n v,out ω = |B ω | 2 , (4.29) n out −ω = |β −ω | 2 + |B −ω | 2 = n u,out ω + n v.out ω . (4.30)

From these relations one sees that the norm of β ω fixes the occupation number of outgoing phonons spontaneously produced by the scattering of the quantum field near the black hole horizon. In the standard analysis without dispersion, n u,out ω is Planckian and describes the Hawking effect [32]. In that case, for massless conformally invariant fields, n v,out ω , the number of left moving phonons spontaneously produced, identically vanishes. In the presence of dispersion, one generally finds n v,out ω ≪ n u,out ω . Hence the scattering of a dispersive field on a sonic horizon basically consists of a more general and slightly modified version of the standard Hawking effect. This is true when ω max 2κ. For larger values of κ, the deviations become large, but the structure of Eq. (4.22) and the meaning of its coefficients remain unchanged [131,158].

Note that, if we choose a quantum state |χ which is not an eigenstate of the initial occupation operatorsn i,in ω , Eq. (4.27) is not complete and other terms must be added. For instance, if |χ = 1 √ 2 â u,in † ω +â v,in † ω |0 in . (4.31) the final occupation number of the u mode would be where the cross term Re(α ω A * ω ) is missing. In that case, Eq. (4.27) becomes rather complicated and it is difficult to have a grasp on the expected particle content of the out modes. Let us imagine what would happen in the case in which the horizon is not always present but it is created at some finite time as in [9]. In that case, at early times, there are only two ABM for each frequency ω, because the negative norm modes are not present, since the condensate is everywhere supersonic. If the initial state was the Fock vacuum for these two modes, once the horizon was formed, one would reasonably expect that this quantum state would look as "almost" vacuum for the destruction operatorsâ u,in ω ,â v,in ω . More difficult would be to determine whether the state would also be vacuum forâ in −ω . It is of course reasonable to assume that, if the horizon was formed adiabatically, the state would not contain negative frequency particles. If this were the case, the results of [131] for a stationary geometry with vacuum initial condition might be applicable also to a realistic scenario when the horizon is created at a finite time. 1 2 |α ω + A ω | 2 + β 2 = 1 2 + 3 2 |β| 2 + Re(α ω A * ω ),(4. In [41], a numerical simulation shows that this is indeed what happens when the state is initially vacuum and the horizon is formed. After some transient radiation, the emission settles down to a stationary regime, coinciding with what is expected from calculations in eternal black holes (see Sec. 3.3.2). Probably, even in a real experiment where both the initial state are not so under control and the formation of the horizon cannot be made arbitrary slow, things would not differ so much from this situation. The initial state would almost coincide with the eternal in vacuum state and standard Hawking radiation would be propably detected, after some transient period, in the same fashion as in numerical simulations [41].

However, it is meaningful to ask what would happen in a real black hole if one allowed for modified superluminal dispersion relation. In general relativity a quantum state that is vacuum before the star collapse at late times is equivalent, at late times, to the Unruh state [182] of an eternal black hole. In our language, this corresponds to say that the quantum state which is vacuum before the star collapse would be equivalent to the vacuum state described above (n u,in ω = n v,in ω = n in −ω = 0) for a stationary geometry. Would this be also true for superluminal modifications to the dispersion relation? What would the corresponding occupation number n in −ω be? Would this quantum state show Hawking radiation at late times? In general relativity the Unruh state is the only possible one. If the collapse tried to select another state, vacuum polarization and energy carried by Hawking radiation would not cancel each other on the horizon, leading there to a divergence of the stress energy tensor, so that the back reaction would likely prevent the formation of the horizon [14]. So the Unruh state is the only possible state for real black holes and it must be stable against perturbations of initial data, since other possibilities would eventually yield divergences on the horizon.

In the presence of superluminal dispersion relation the situation is completely different. First, the divergence on the horizon is tamed by the modifications of the dispersion relation. In fact, modes traced back in time toward the horizon are still blueshifted but not infinitely, because when they reach a certain wavenumber they can cross the horizon and propagate inside the horizon. This leads to the second difference: Playing now the movie forward in time, modes can escape from the horizon carrying information about the interior and, in the case of a real black hole, about the singularity. As a consequence one should put conditions on the singularity (or on a very strong curvature region, assuming that the superluminal dispersion relation tamed also the singularity) in order to predict what one would observe at late times far from the black hole. In any case, it is not clear at all why the quantum state should appear as the equivalent of the Unruh state [that is the state considered above in Eq. (4.28), (4.29), and (4.30)] and, for instance, not as the equivalent of the Boulware state [30], with no emission of particle, or something in between.

Observables

In what follows we study, for a stationary geometry, the properties of n out as a function of ω, for different values of D, keeping fixed all the other parameters. When working with a relativistic massless field, one would obtain a Planck spectrum with a temperature T H = κ K /2π (solid line). When D is sufficiently large (D 0.1), the phonon flux does not differ from the Planck flux at the corresponding temperature. However, for D = 0.1, ω max is much smaller than the Hawking temperature, such that this cut off occurs at a frequency much lower than T H . In that case the flux is much weaker than the corresponding Planckian one. However, the spectrum is remarkably close to the Planckian spectrum when ω max is sufficiently large and, even when it differs from it, it changes very smoothly with D, becoming weaker but with the same shape. This is in accordance with the robustness of the flux already recognized in [184,33,46]. Since comparing energy fluxes f ω is not convenient, we introduce the temperature function, implicitly defined by n u,out ω ≡ 1 exp(ω/T ω ) − 1 . (4.35) As such, T ω is simply another way to express n u,out ω , but it presents the great advantage of being constant whenever the flux is Planckian.

In fact, as can be seen in Fig. 4.3, right panel, T ω remains remarkably constant, until ω approaches the critical frequency ω max of Eq. (4.17). At that frequency, the signal drops down as it must do, since For ω max 10 T H , as can be seen from the horizontal upper scale, these spectra are, to a high precision, Planckian. In both panels, the fixed parameters are Λ/κ = 15, n = n p = 1.

the Bogoliubov transformation (4.22) becomes trivial, because the negative norm mode ϕ −ω no longer exists above ω max . This cutoff effect relies on the asymptotic value of the flow v + c, and has been studied in detail in [132,131]. In what follows we focus instead on the deviations of the spectrum which depend on the near horizon properties of v + c. Furthermore, from Fig. 4.3, it is even clearer what we observed for the flux f ω : The spectrum is close to a Planckian one even for very small values of D (and, as a consequence, of ω max ) but with a much lower value of the temperature. This robustness of the thermal properties of the spectrum was completely unexpected and can actually be properly investigated only with numerical techniques. In Sec. 4.3, we will show that the robustness of the thermality of the spectrum is a very general property and its temperature, although possibly very different from the standard Hawking temperature, can be estimated in a simple way as an average of the standard decay rate (4.2) over a small region around the horizon [see Eq. (4.54)].

Since one of our main goals is to identify the parameters governing the deviations from Planckianity, it is convenient to define ∆ H ≡ f ω=T H − f T H ω=T H f T H ω=T H ,(4.36) where f ω is the energy flux defined in Eq. (4.34) and f T H ω is the thermal flux at temperature T H . This quantity parametrizes the deviation of the observed flux f at a reference frequency ω = T H , with respect to the standard thermal spectrum f T H at temperature T H . In Fig. 4.4, ∆ H is plotted as a function of ω max /κ for two values of D. As shown in Eq. (4.17), ω max depends both on Λ and D, so, for each curve, ω max /κ is varied by varying only Λ and keeping D constant. When ω max /κ 2, that is ω max is an order of magnitude larger than the Hawking temperature T H , there are no substantial deviations of the observed flux from the Planckian one. Furthermore the two curves merge together, signaling that those small deviations are governed only by the ratio ω max /κ and not independently by D or Λ. This means that, as far as this analysis allow to discriminate, all the effects of dispersion are encoded in a single parameter ω max .

All the above results [131] were obtained using the symmetrical profiles of Eq. (4.3). One may therefore wonder whether the observed robustness of the spectrum is not partially due to the symmetry of the flow with respect to the horizon, namely w(−x) = −w(x). Furthermore, using a velocity profile as simple as that of Eq. (4.3), some difficulties appear in the characterization of the deviations of the observed spectrum from a Planckian one at temperature T H . In fact, these deviations have different origins and are governed by different parameters. To investigate these two issues, in the following sections we shall consider flows which are highly asymmetric and with short scales perturbations.

ASYMMETRIC VELOCITY PROFILES

Asymmetric velocity profiles

To investigate whether the robustness of the spectrum observed in [131] was due or not to the symmetry of the flow with respect to the horizon, we study here a more general class of flows with respect to those of Eq. and, for the sake of simplicity, we investigate only the case n = 1. In particular, we shall study the spectrum in the interesting limiting cases where the horizon disappears because the flux becomes either sub or supersonic.

The new parameter D 1 , which was taken to be 0 in [132,131], fixes the asymmetry between sub and supersonic regimes. When D 1 > D, the flow is everywhere subsonic and, when D 1 < −D, it is supersonic, see Fig. 4.5. Instead, when |D 1 | < D, there is a sonic (Killing) horizon where w = 0, localized at x H = − Dc H κ arctanh D 1 D . (4.38) It separates the sub (x > x H ) from the supersonic region (x < x H ). In such a flow, when ignoring dispersion, the spectrum of upstream phonons spontaneously emitted from the horizon would be very simple, and would strictly correspond to Hawking radiation [183,184]. It would follow a Planck law at Hawking temperature T H = κ K /2π, where the surface gravity κ K is κ K ≡ ∂ x (c + v)| x=xH = κ 1 − D 1 D 2 . (4.39)

Note that, as for the symmetric velocity profiles of Eq. (4.3), κ still fixes the maximum slope of w(x), but it cannot now be interpreted as the surface gravity of the horizon, that is determined by κ K . In Fig. 4.6, T ω and T H (horizontal lines) are plotted for various values of D 1 . They are both normalized using the temperature scale κ/2π, determined by κ, in order to better appreciate the scaling of κ K with D 1 , which is given in Eq. (4.39). We distinguish four regimes:

• For |D 1 | sufficiently smaller than D, the spectrum is Planckian until ω approaches ω max , where it vanishes. For D 1 > 0, ω max decreases since |w(x = −∞)| does so, and conversely for D 1 < 0. However for both signs, at low frequency T ω closely follows κ K /2π of (4.39). • When |D 1 | → D, κ K of (4.39) drops down to 0 and the sonic horizon disappears. In this critical regime, deviations from Planckianity appear even at low frequency.

• For D 1 > D, the flow is everywhere subsonic. There is no particle production because there are no negative norm modes with ω > 0.

• For D 1 < −D, the flow is supersonic. A new critical frequency ω min < ω max appears. For 0 < ω < ω min , there are now 4 asymptotic in and out modes, and the scattering matrix is 4 × 4, and the treatment of Sec. 4.1.2 does not apply. However, for ω min < ω < ω max there are only 3 modes, this frequency band can be handled by this analysis. In this range, as can be seen in the left panel, n u,out ω is close to the case where D 1 > −D, even though the spectrum is not at all Planckian.

Furthermore, we checked that the shape of the spectrum is hardly changed when varying the "healing frequency" Λ. Namely, spectra calculated with different values of ξ almost coincide when T ω is plotted versus the rescaled quantity ω/ω max (Λ).

Finally, to identify the parameters governing the deviation from Planckianity, ∆ H , defined in Eq. (4.36), is plotted as a function of ω max (Λ)/κ in Fig. 4.6 (right panel). Various values of D 1 are used, while the ratio D 1 /D is kept constant respectively for the three lower curves (D 1 /D = −1/5), and for the three upper curves (D 1 /D = 4/5). As expected, ∆ H goes to 0 for large values of ω max /κ. What we learn here is that the leading deviations from Planckianity do not depend separately on D 1 or D but only on their ratio.

To conclude, we stress that the thermal spectrum at the naïve surface gravity (4.39) provides a reliable approximation of the actual phonon spectrum in all cases but the critical ones where the sonic horizon disappears. In these extreme cases, the spectrum is no longer thermal and its properties are governed by the dispersive properties of field.

Short-scale perturbations: Broadening the horizon

As discussed at the beginning of this chapter, the robustness of Hawking radiation against modifications of the dispersion relation is well established. However, it is not well known for which flow configurations this is true, since only very smooth and symmetric flow profiles have been studied so far, with the exception of the step-like profile of [158]. In this case, somehow unexpectedly, the predicted spectrum is thermal even if the Hawking temperature cannot be computed because the surface gravity is virtually infinite because of the non-smooth profile. To understand why this is possible, we consider velocity profiles with quite sharp perturbations on very small scales and, varying the velocity profile, we study the deviations of the spectrum from Planckianity and, in the case the spectrum is thermal, the deviation of its temperature from Hawking one.

Profiles

As already mentioned, it is difficult to characterize the deviations from the Planck spectrum by using a velocity profile described by such few parameters as that of Eq. (4.3).

To this aim, we introduce flow profiles governed by several scales of the form c(x) + v(x) = w(x) = w B (x) + w p (x),(4.40) where w B is a reference background profile and w p a perturbation of smaller amplitude: 0 ≤ |w p (x)| |w B (x)|. The background profile is still given by Eq. (4.3) w B (x) c H = D sign(x) tanh 1/n κ B |x| c H D n . (4.41)

We shall consider two classes of perturbations, symmetric ones with respect to the horizon, i.e., w p (x) = −w p (−x), and then, in Sec. 4.3.7, more general ones without that symmetry. The symmetric ones are similar to w B : w p (x) c H = D p sign(x) tanh 1/np κ p |x| c H D p np . (4.42)

The condition 0 ≤ |w p | |w B | is simply 0 ≤ D p D. In Fig. 4.7, we plot w(x), its first and second derivatives for different values of κ p /κ B and D p .

The smoothness parameter n p will range from 0.5 to 4. We shall see that it only affects the flux marginally. In fact the most important quantity is D p . Its role is to fix the interval of x over which w(x) is linear. In this respect, it should be noticed that the surface gravity is equal to |x lin | ≃ c H D p κ p ,(4.κ K ≡ ∂ x (c + v)| x=0 = κ B + κ p ,(4.44) irrespectively of the value of D p , and that of n p . For relativistic fields, D p would play no role since the temperature is κ K /2π (when ignoring gray body factors, a correct approximation in our settings when q = 1/2). For dispersive fields instead, D p plays a crucial role.

Planckian character

In Fig. 4.8, left panel, we present the energy flux of Eq. (4.34) as a function of ω for different values of the D p , keeping fixed all the other parameters. When working with a relativistic massless field, one would obtain a Planck spectrum with a temperature T H = κ K /2π, irrespectively of the value of D p . To ease the comparison with the dispersive fluxes, we have represented this flux by a solid line. Working with the Bogoliubov-de Gennes equation (3.19), we see instead that the flux varies with D p . In fact it increases monotonically. Moreover, when D p → 0, we verified that it coincides with the flux one would obtain in the reference flow w B . We also see that when D p is sufficiently large, the flux saturates and agrees to a high accuracy with the Planck spectrum at temperature T H , in accordance with the robustness [184,33,46] of the flux, as discussed in Sec
T 0 ≡ lim ω→0 T ω ,(4.46) we study the relative deviation ∆ T (ω) ≡ T 0 − T ω T 0 . (4.47) For all values of D p we found that |∆ T (T 0 )| 3. × 10 −4 . (4.48)

Note that ∆ T (T 0 ) = 0 is not due to numerical errors, but it really characterizes the deviations. Equation (4.48) implies that, to a high accuracy, the spectrum remains Planckian even when T 0 completely differs from T H . (In the present case, it differs by 33%, since κ K = 1.5 κ B .) The smallness of ∆ T (T 0 ) also implies that the parameters that govern the deviations from Planckianity differ from those governing the shift T 0 − T H . Hence when studying the robustness of the Hawking flux against introducing dispersion, one must differentiate these two types of deviations. These are the first important results of [69]. They generalize what was observed in [132,131] to a much wider class of flows: First, that the thermality is well preserved whenever ω max /T B 10, and, second, that the relative temperature shift T 0 /T H − 1 is much larger than ∆ T (T 0 ). In the sequel we work with ω max /T B 10 and study how the low frequency temperature T 0 depends on κ p , D p , n p and Λ.

The critical value of D p

In Fig. 4.8, right panel, we saw that T 0 starts from κ B /2π for D p → 0, and asymptotes to T H = κ K /2π for D p larger than some critical value D crit p . (We have checked that this is also the case when κ p < 0, and this down to κ p = −κ B , in which case the surface gravity κ K vanishes.) To understand what fixes D crit p , we first consider series of flows w B + w p (D p ) for different values of κ p at fixed κ B . For each series, the surface gravity κ K = κ B + κ p is thus constant. The resulting temperatures T 0 (D p ) are presented in Fig. 4.9, left panel. This figure reveals many interesting features. For small values of D p , T 0 increases linearly with D p in a manner essentially independent of κ p . In addition, for each series, when D p is large enough, T 0 saturates at the standard result T H = κ K /2π. Given the linear behavior of T 0 for small D p and the saturation for large values, i.e., 2πT 0 (D p ≪ D crit p ) ∼ κ B + s D p , 2πT 0 (D p ≫ D crit p ) ∼ κ B + κ p = κ K ,(4.ω Mp ≃ Λ κ K κ p D p 3/2 . (4.52) Since the deviations from the standard result should be small when ω Mp /κ K ≫ 1, the critical value of D p below which the temperature of the emitted radiation is no longer given by T H should scale as D crit p ∼ κ p (κ K Λ 2 ) 1/3 .(4.53) A rigorous study [50] of the connection formulas [33,45,94,187] confirms the result of this simple reasoning. Equation (4.53) is in agreement with Eq. (4.51) for κ p ≪ κ B . In the following section, we shall numerically validate it for all values of κ p . Before proceeding, we checked that D crit p does not significantly depend on the background quantities n, D and κ B , as one could have expected. The weak, subleading, dependence on n p is studied in Sec. 4.3.5.

The averaged surface gravity

So far we have some understanding of the Planckian character of the flux in the robust regime when D p > D crit p , and of the value of D crit p [50]. Instead, what fixes T ω outside this regime, for D p < D crit p , is terra incognita.

We shall now numerically establish that, to a good approximation when ω max /T B 10, the temperature is determined by the average of the gradient dw/dx over a width that we parametrize by d ξ = d L ξ +d R ξ , where d L ξ and d R ξ are respectively the width on the left and on the right calculated from the Killing horizon at x = 0.

In fact, given that the dispersion of Eq. (3.22) defines a "healing length" ξ = √ 2c/Λ, it is not surprising that ξ in turns defines a minimal resolution length, such that details of w(x) smaller than d ξ are not "seen" by the phonon field. In other words, because of dispersion, it is as if we were dealing with a horizon of width d ξ . 3 Moreover, when d L ξ = d R ξ , this means that the center of the effective horizon is displaced with respect to the Killing horizon tox hor = (d R ξ − d L ξ )/2. To test the idea that the temperature be determined by an average "surface gravity," we introducē κ ≡ 1 d ξ d R ξ −d L ξ dx dw(x) dx = w(d R ξ ) − w(−d L ξ ) d ξ . (4.54)

Using Eqs. (4.41) and (4.42), we get κ = κ B + w p (d R ξ ) + w p (d L ξ ) d ξ + O κ B d ξ Dc H 2n ,(4.55) where we assume that d ξ ≪ D/κ B , so that the average of the background term w B is approximately κ B , plus corrections of the order of (κ B d ξ /Dc H ) 2n . We proceed as follows: We compute T 0 for about 200 values of D p for some fixed values of the perturbed flow parameters (n p , κ p ) and the dispersive scale Λ, i.e., along series as those represented in Fig. 4.9. Out of this set we extract five quantities, namely, κ B (fit) , n p (fit) , κ p (fit) , d L ξ , and d R ξ , by fittinḡ κ(κ B , n p , κ p , d L ξ , d R ξ ; D p )/2π of Eq. (4.55) with a nonlinear least-squares method. The agreement between the couples T 0 (D p ) and the fitted functionκ(D p )/2π is really striking-see Fig. 4.10 for an example.

Moreover, the fitted values κ B (fit) and κ p (fit) are in very good agreement (less than 1.5% and 1%, respectively) with their values used in the flow w. This is a necessary check to validate that it is the average of ∂ x w that governs the temperature. However, the agreement between n p (fit) and n p is less good. This indicates that the actual temperature is not exactly given by the average of Eq. (4.54). 4 Even though this is a subdominant effect, it is interesting to see under which conditions a better agreement between n p (fit) and n p is reached.

Subleading effects in n p

The parameter n p of Eq. of n p make that transition sharper. Therefore higher derivatives of w will become very large at the end of the linear-regime region, near x lin of Eq. (4.43). As a consequence, nonadiabatic effects become more important, thereby generating larger deviations between T 0 andκ/2π of Eq. (4.55).

To investigate these deviations, we compare T 0 (D p ) for various values of n p . As it appears from Fig. 4.11, left panel, s and D crit p do not significantly depend on n p . However, the transition region between the linear regime and the constant regime depends on n p . The transition is indeed monotonic for small values of n p whereas, for n p > 1, T 0 oscillates around T H = κ K /2π. The monotonic function of Eq. (4.54) will of course never describe these oscillations. However, for n p ≤ 1, one can compare n p (fit) with n p . The result is shown in Fig. 4.11, right panel. The dots and the squares are computed using different values of Λ/κ B , respectively 15 and 20. One sees that n p fit is always larger than n p . One also sees that the discrepancy becomes smaller when n p decreases, as expected, since nonadiabatic effects are smaller for smoother profiles, hence smaller n p . One should not overestimate the relevance of this discrepancy because we found that the value of n p (fit) is correlated in the fitting procedure to the difference d R ξ − d L ξ . For instance, when imposing d R ξ = d L ξ , n p (fit) − n p is reduced by a factor of about 2. The important conclusion is the following. When nonadiabatic effects are small, the temperature of the phonon flux is well approximated by 1/2π times the average of ∂ x w over a certain width d ξ [see Eq. (4.54)].

The scaling laws of the width d ξ

Unlike κ B , κ p , n p , D p which characterize the flow w and Λ which fixes the healing length, d ξ is not a parameter entering the mode equation (A.97), which is directly derived from the Bogoliubov-de Gennes equation (3.19). Therefore the value obtained in a fit cannot be compared with some a priori known value. In fact d ξ should be conceived as an emergent, effective, length scale.

Before using numerical results to determine how d ξ scales when changing κ K , κ p and Λ, we point out that we can predict how it should do, as d ξ is closely related to D crit p of Eq. (4.53). Indeed, taking the limits of Eq. (4.55) for small and large D p we obtain κ D p ≪ κ p d ξ c H ∼ κ B + 2D p c H d ξ , κ D p ≫ κ p d ξ c H ∼ κ K ,(4.56) and hence, using Eq. (4.49) and (4.50), we get d ξ = 2c H D crit p κ p . (4.57) In other words, the critical value of D p separating the two regimes of T 0 (D p ) shown in Fig. 4.10 strictly corresponds to the width d ξ one should use in Eq. (4.54) to follow T 0 (D p ). Using Eq. (4.53), we get d ξ = d × c H (κ K Λ 2 ) 1/3 ,(4.58) where d is a constant. In terms of the healing length ξ = √ 2c H /Λ, this gives d ξ ξ = d × 2ξ d K −1/3 ,(4.59) where d K ≡ c H /κ K is the surface gravity length. Hence, when ξ ≪ d K , d ξ is significantly larger than ξ. The prediction of Eq. (4.58) can be numerically validated by a linear 5 least-square fit of where the numerical error is on the last figure. The exponent a differs from about 5% from its expected value −0.667. The exponent b is also close to its expected value 0. Finally, c perfectly matches its 5 When the function is linear in the parameters, a close-form solution of the χ 2 minimization problem is available. This method is therefore preferable to non-linear ones. 6 Because of numerical noise, for some series (Λ,κp), it was not possible to obtain a sufficiently large number of points (T 0 , Dp). In particular it is difficult to obtain T 0 for large values of Dp. We therefore decided to use only the series for which we obtained T 0 for every value of Dp from 0 to 0.1 (namely 100 points, with step 0.001). In this way, there are points with Dp D crit p in the series (T 0 , Dp) used to determine d ξ . This condition is satisfied by 71 series over 110. In brief, for the considered class of flows, we have established that, to some accuracy, log d ξ κ B c H = log(d) + a log Λ κ B + b log κ p κ B + c log 1 + κ p κ B . • the spectrum is Planckian,

• the temperature equalsκ/2π for a width d ξ , and

• d ξ scales according to Eq. (4.58) with d ≈ 1.6.

From our fits we also found that the broadened horizon is slightly displaced with respect to the Killing horizon by a shift |x hor | ≈ 0.1d ξ . However, because of the symmetry of Eq. (4.55), we cannot know the sign ofx hor . To determine it, it is necessary to consider asymmetric flows.

Asymmetric flow profiles

In this section, we consider profiles of the form w pδ (x) = w p (x − δ) − w p (−δ),(4.62) which are simply w p of Eq. (4.42) shifted from the horizon by δ. We have subtracted the constant term w p (−δ) so that w B + w pδ still vanishes at x = 0. In Fig. 4.12, T 0 is plotted as a function of D p for different δ (left plot), and as a function of δ for different D p (right plot). The behavior of these curves is quite complicated. To understand it, we first consider smooth perturbations, i.e. perturbations with D p > D crit p ≈ 0.06. From the right panel of Fig. 4.12, when D p > D crit p , but for both signs of δ, we see that T 0 monotonically interpolates from T H = κ K /2π, when δ is small and the perturbation is close to the horizon, to T B = κ B /2π, when δ → ∞. In that case, the perturbation is far from the horizon and thus no longer contributes to κ K which equals its background value κ B . When considering instead well localized perturbations, that is values of D p smaller than D crit p , we see that T 0 oscillates, but only for δ sufficiently negative, i.e. when the perturbation is sufficiently displaced in the region where the decaying mode oscillates. 7 The origin of these oscillations can be understood as follows. The scattering on the well localized bump in ∂ x w(x) interferes with the scattering on the horizon, thereby producing oscillatory behaviors. 8 It is quite clear that the average of Eq. (4.54) cannot describe these oscillations. However this should not be conceived as limiting the validity of the observation that the temperature is given by this average. Indeed, these oscillations result from interferences between two spatially separated scatterings. In these circumstances it is meaningless to apply Eq. (4.54), which is based on the near horizon properties of w.

In addition, when oscillations are found for T 0 as a function of D p or δ, the Planck character of the spectrum is also lost. Indeed, the parameter ∆ T (ω) of Eq. (4.47) characterizing deviations from Planckianity also displays oscillations (of relative amplitude ∼ 20%) (see Fig. 4.13, left panel). As can be seen, the number of peaks increases with increasing values of −δ. This is to be expected since more "room" is available to fit resonating modes. In fact this phenomenon can be viewed as a precursor of the black hole laser effect [47]. If the perturbation were strong enough to behave as a white horizon, the resonating modes would start growing exponentially [49,68] as shown in Chap. 6. In that case complexfrequency modes appear when the distance between the horizons is sufficiently large, and their number increases with that distance.

To complete this analysis of the flows of Eq. (4.62), we verified thatκ of Eq. (4.54) still approximately governs the temperature. In Fig. 4.13 (right panel), both the numerical data (dots) and the fitted function (solid line) are reported for the series T 0 (δ), with D p = 0.05 and 0.15, of Fig. 4.12 (right panel). This function is obtained as a two-variable function of δ and D p by using the numerical data of T 0 . The fitting region is restricted to those values of δ for which there are no oscillations and for three values of D p , namely D p = 0.05, 0.1, 0.15. As a reference, the dotted line represents κ K (δ)/2π, i.e. the temperature one would obtain without dispersion, for D p = 0.05. As a check, we also fitted the values of κ B , κ p and n p and compared them with the corresponding constants used in the numerical analysis. The relative difference is very small, respectively, around 0.1%, 1% and 5%.

As one can see in Fig. 4.13 (right panel), the agreement between the actual temperature T 0 and the average surface gravityκ/2π is quite good in the parameter region where there are no interference effects. One can also see that for D p = 0.05,κ/2π of Eq. (4.54) follows T 0 much more closely than the surface gravity κ K (δ)/2π. In summary, the analysis of asymmetrical perturbations first indicates that the shiftx hor is always negative. That is, the broadened horizon is displaced towards the region where the decaying mode oscillates. Moreover we find that the value of the shift does not depend directly on δ. By this we mean thatx hor depends on δ only because d ξ of Eq. (4.58) is a function of δ through κ K (δ). Second, for sufficiently smooth perturbations we found that the temperature is still approximately given by the average of Eq. (4.54). Finally, when the perturbation is sharp and sufficiently deep inside the supersonic region, we found that the spectrum is affected by interferences and explained why it is so. x w p (x) −A A −∆ ∆ 2π/k Z θ/k Z

Undulations

Supersonic flows, possessing a sonic horizon with a negative surface gravity κ K , behave like the time reverse of black hole horizons: long wavelength modes are blueshifted when scattered on such a horizon. Thanks to dispersion, the blueshift effect saturates and, as a result, the scattering matrix is well-defined. Moreover, for each ω, this matrix is closely related to Eq. (4.22) evaluated in the corresponding black hole flow [132,131]. In spite of this correspondence, it has been recently observed that white hole flows display a specific phenomenon related to the limit ω → 0 [138,209]. Indeed, these flows produce a zero-frequency mode, a stationary undulation, which has a finite wavelength k Z and a high amplitude.

To schematically describe such undulation and study its impact on the emitted spectrum, we consider the following perturbations: w p (x) c H = A sin (k Z x − θ) e −(x/∆) 4 .(4

.63)

A is the amplitude of the undulation, and θ fixes its phase on the horizon at x = 0. The new length ∆ is taken to be larger than 1/k Z , and comparable to Dc H /κ B which characterizes the linear regime of the background flow w B (see Fig. 4.14 for a graphical representation). In Fig. 4.15, T 0 is plotted versus θ for different values of k Z , keeping fixed A k Z so that the surface gravity of Eq. (4.44) does not change with k Z . Indeed one gets κ K (θ) = κ B + A k Z cos(θ). When k Z ≫ 1/d ξ , as expected from our previous results, the temperature is very close to κ B /2π, that of the background flow w B . Hence, independently of the value of θ, the perturbation is completely washed out by dispersion. On the contrary, when the wavelength is larger than the width of the horizon, i.e. when k Z < 2π/d ξ ≈ 30κ B /c H , we expect that 2π T 0 will be approximately given by κ K (θ) given above. This can be observed for k Z equal to 5 κ B /c H . One notices that the curve is not exactly symmetric around For these values, δ Z = 2π/k Z is comparable to d ξ . Hence the undulation can enter into resonance with the scattering on the horizon, thereby producing a kind of parametric amplification of the flux. This can be observed for θ = 0 and k Z = 10κ B /c H where the temperature is larger than κ K /2π = 1.5T B . The fact that this resonance occurs for wavelengths ∼ d ξ , reinforces the fact that the effective width of the horizon is indeed d ξ .

Summary and discussion

By considering series of supersonic flows consisting of local perturbations w p defined on the top of a background flow w B , we were able to identify the parameters that fix the spectral properties of Bogoliubov phonons emitted by a (black hole) sonic horizon.

We showed that the flux remains Planckian to high accuracy, see Eq. (4.48), even when the temperature strongly differs from T H = κ K /2π, the standard value fixed by the surface gravity, see Fig. 4.8, right panel. This result implies two things. In spite of the fact that each mode of frequency ω has its own turning point of Eq. (4.16), the Planckianity first implies that the temperature function T ω of Eq. (4.35) is not determined by some function of w(x) evaluated at an ω-dependent location, as T ω is common to all low frequencies with respect to ω max of Eq. (4.17). Second, it implies that the parameters governing the small deviations from Planckianity differ from those governing the difference between the low frequency temperature T 0 and T H . In fact, whereas the difference T 0 − T H is governed by the near horizon properties, deviations from Planckianity are governed by inverse powers of ω max /T 0 , where ω max is fixed by D asympt = D + D p , characterizing the global properties of the supersonic transition region, such as the asymptotic velocity excess.

Moreover, in contradistinction to the relativistic case where the temperature is locally fixed by κ K of Eq. (4.44), we show that the temperature T 0 is determined, to a high accuracy when ω max /T H 10, by a nonlocal quantity: the spatial average across the horizon of ∂ x w over a width d ξ , as if the horizon were broadened. 9 Hence, whenever w(x) hardly varies over d ξ in the near horizon region,κ of Eq. (4.54) equals the surface gravity κ K , and the standard temperature is found. Instead, when w varies on scales shorter than d ξ , T 0 is no longer the standard one, but it is well approximated byκ/2π for all frequencies ω ≪ ω max . This result is reinforced by the fact that the effective horizon width d ξ scales according to a well-defined law given in Eq. (4.58). Under this new perspective, the findings of [158], where the emitted spectrum is still Planckian are not surprising. In fact the averageκ is well defined and finite, even in the extremal case in which the surface gravity κ K diverges and the Hawking temperature T H is not definite. Furthermore, the above results are basically valid for all flows. They cease to be valid under the same restricted conditions, namely when the flow contains a sharp perturbation well localized far enough from the horizon. Then the perturbation induces a scattering that interferes with the Hawking effect, thereby engendering oscillations. These are similar to those found in inflationary spectra, and are also attributable to interferences between two scatterings. It is clear that the local average of Eq. (4.54) cannot describe these oscillations which are nonlocal on a scale much larger than the width d ξ . Hence we can say that when ω max /T H 10 and when the scattering essentially arises near the horizon, the spectrum is near Planckian, and with a temperature approximately given byκ of Eq. (4.54). Finally, to mimic what is found in white hole flows [138,209], we studied profiles containing an undulation on the top of a smooth profile. By varying its wavelength, we have found three different behaviors that match the above analysis.

To conclude, we stress that, even if these results on acoustic black holes in realistic BEC flows are quite solid, further analyses are required in order to apply those arguments to real black holes, with modified superluminal dispersion relation modelled on the phononic one. As discussed in Sec. 4.1.2, the situation is complicated by the fact that a black hole is originated by a star collapse which might not naturally select an Unruh-like state, because the superluminal dispersion relation would tame any divergence on the horizons for non-Unruh states. Moreover, the presence of a singularity or of a strong curvature region would not be hidden beyond the horizon because there would exist modes that would be able to cross the horizon outward. Thus, fixing the boundary conditions in this strong curvature region would be quite problematic. There is no a priori reason why, after the collapse, the initial vacuum state should appear as if the modes coming out from the singularity were in their vacuum state.

WARP DRIVES IN BOSE-EINSTEIN CONDENSATES

One of the main objectives of this Thesis is to show the importance of analogue gravity as a set of tools for the investigation of interesting problems both in condensed matter and in general relativity. The analysis of this chapter is a significant example of this twofold usefulness of analogue models of gravity. In fact, we extend here the analysis of Chap. 4 to Bose-Einstein condensates (BECs) with two horizons, separating three regions: one internal subsonic region and two external supersonic ones. The coupling of a black and a white acoustic horizon introduces new interesting and nontrivial effects with respect to the single horizon scenario. First, this system is mildly unstable, showing a linear divergence (in time) of the detection rate measured by a detector coupled with density fluctuations. Second, in this framework, one can study the limiting case in which the two horizons collapse into a single point. In that case, the horizons and the subsonic region disappear. Interestingly, some particle production is still present, confirming the first results of [70], described in Sec. 4.2.

Furthermore, the acoustic metrics associated with this class of flows have the same features of the warpdrive spacetimes investigated in Chap. 2. However, in the present system, the phonon field is described by a dispersion relation that naturally shows supersonic modifications at large frequencies. Thus, this analysis allows to learn some lessons about how the instability found in Chap. 2 would be affected by trans-Planckian physics in the case a superluminal modification of the dispersion relation of quantum fields took place at trans-Planckian frequencies. This sort of studies is of fundamental importance because, as broadly discussed in Chap. 2, the possibility of violating causality (e.g. of realizing time travel) is strictly related to spacetime shortcuts allowing faster-than-light travel [60]. In spacetimes described by analogue metrics closed timelike curves are forbidden because these geometries inherit the property of stable causality from the underlying Newtonian "world" endowed with an absolute time. Nevertheless, it is interesting to understand if chronology is protected by some more fundamental mechanism even in spacetimes that do not emerge from a Galilean invariant system. In particular, the result of the present chapter is in favor of the instability of dynamical warp drives, even in the presence of superluminal dispersion relations. However, this instability is only linear and it is therefore much milder than the exponential growth of the renormalized stress-energy tensor for scalar fields with relativistic dispersion relation.

Mode analysis and scattering matrix c(x) + v(x) = w(x) = −c H D sign(x 2 − L 2 ) tanh 1/n κ B |x + L| c H D n tanh 1/n κ W |x − L| c H D n . (5.1) We define regions I, II, and III as the three regions with x < −L, −L < x < L, and x > L, respectively. In region II w > 0, i.e., the fluid is subsonic. On the contrary, in region I and III w < 0 and the fluid is supersonic. Fig. 3.2). When the flow is supersonic, we found four propagating modes with real wavenumber, while, when the flow is subsonic, in addition to the two standard solutions with real k, there are two roots with complex wavevector, respectively corresponding to decaying and growing modes at x → +∞ (vice versa for x → −∞). Furthermore, in an acoustic single black-hole geometry, the presence of those two extra-roots (real in the supersonic region and complex in the subsonic one) generates three globally defined asymptotically bounded modes (ABM) for each value of the Killing frequency ω.

In the present situation, where the flow is subsonic in the compact region II and supersonic in the two asymptotic external regions I and III, the number of ABM sharing the same conserved frequency ω is 4. Indeed, in regions I and III, there are 4 propagating modes with real wavenumber. Moreover, all the four solutions of the dispersion relation in the subsonic region II are physically acceptable. In fact, also the two modes with complex wavenumber are bounded, because of the compactness of region II.

Indeed, let us remark that β (2) ω is related to a single scattering on the white horizon (x = L), while β (1) ω is related to a double scattering process, first on the black horizon (x = −L) and eventually on the white horizon. Because of their different origins, those two terms contribute very differently to the total spectrum of emitted particles, as shown in the following section.

Numerical analysis

Results

We present here the results of a numerical computation of the coefficients of the 4 × 4 scattering matrix [Eq. (5.3)], performed by a modified version of the code used for the analysis of Chap. 4 (see Appendix B.2.2). Since the β ω coefficient of a single black hole geometry is now generalized by the two β (1) ω and β (2) ω , we focus only on these two coefficients, because they provide the most relevant physical information about the spontaneous emission of particles in a black-hole-white-hole geometry. As described in the previous section, they indeed represent the rate of mode conversion into the positive norm outgoing mode φ u,out ω of the incoming negative norm modes (ϕ (i),in −ω ) * . In Chap. 4 we proved that |β ω | 2 follows a Planckian distribution up to a certain frequency ω max , by plotting the effective temperature T ω in Fig. 4.3, which in fact remains constant when ω < ω max . In analogy with the definition of T ω given in Eq. (1) ω | 2 (solid line) and ω|β (2) ω | 2 (dashed line). |β (2) ω | 2 behaves like a standard β-coefficient in a single horizon geometry, while |β (1) ω | 2 diverges like 1/ω 2 . In both panels, the fixed parameters are Λ/κ = 2, n = 1, D = 0.5, κ W = κ B = κ, Lκ/c H = 4. In the left panel of Fig. 5.3, we plot T (1) ω | 2 (solid line) and ω|β (2) ω | 2 (dashed line). In the limit of ω → 0, ω|β (2) ω | 2 goes to a constant for ω → 0, accordingly to the asymptotic behavior of the Planckian distribution |β ω | 2 = 1 e ω/T H − 1 ∼ T H ω (5.10) (in units where = k B = 1), for a constant temperature parameter T (2) ω . Differently, |β (1) ω | 2 diverges as ω −2 , as proved by the constancy of ω 2 |β (1) ω | 2 at low frequencies.

Finally, in Fig. 5.4, we compare ω 2 |β (1) ω | 2 (left panel) and ω|β (2) ω | 2 (right panel), for various values of the distance 2L between the horizons. Note that, for small values of L, the subsonic region between the horizons tends to disappear and the spectrum get strongly modified at low frequencies. This is somehow natural, because low frequencies correspond to long wavelengths. In this case the spectrum is no more Planckian even for the β (2) ω -channel. Nevertheless, some radiation is still present, even in the limiting case L = 0, when there is no horizon. In fact, the presence of two supersonic regions, connected by a non flat compact region around x = 0 seems sufficient to trigger particle production. This result is analogous to what we discovered in [70] and reviewed in Sec. 4.2, for asymmetric supersonic velocity profiles without horizons (see curves with negative D 1 /D in the left panel of Fig. 4.6).

Interpretation

The main result of this chapter is the different scaling of the two β-coefficients: • |β (1) ω | 2 ∼ 1/ω 2 , • |β (2) ω | 2 ∼ 1/ω. We already noticed in the end of Sec. 5.1 that β (1) ω is related to a double scattering on both the horizons. Because of that, β ω | 2 (left panel) and ω|β (2) ω | 2 (right panel), for various values of Lκ/c H . Interestingly, particle production is present even when L = 0 and no horizon is present. In both panels, the fixed parameters are Λ/κ = 2, n = 1, D = 0.5, κ W = κ B = κ. and vice versa. Given that each of those coefficients diverges as ω −1 , their products go as ω −2 . On the contrary, β (2) ω is related to a single scattering on the white horizon. So it must behave as ω −1 just like the β-coefficient in a single horizon geometry.

In this section, we investigate the consequences of the above scalings of the β-coefficients on the flux of emitted particles in a dynamical scenario where the horizons are created at finite time t H = 0. 1 It is well known that a spectrum behaving as ω −1 at low frequency gives rise to a constant emission of particles in time. The question addressed here is what is the effect of the ω −2 scaling of |β (2) ω | 2 . Let us imagine to put a particle detector coupled to the density perturbationρ 1 =ρ − ρ 0 in region III, far from the horizons and after their formation. Using Fermi's golden rule [78], we compute the probability of detecting a quantum of frequency ω per unit of time as a function of the time T elapsed after the formation of the horizons. By definition ofφ in Eq. As a consequence, since the integration over frequencies is performed only on positive values of ω, only those terms of G + related to negative norm modes contribute to the final result. Aiming to measure the production of Hawking quanta in region III, far from the horizons, we rewrite the in modes of Eq. (5.14) in term of out modes. Furthermore, we look only at right-going modes in the right asymptotic region and assume that the detector can distinguish positive norm modes χ u,out ω from negative norm modes χ (1),out −ω . Under these assumptions, P ω d reduces to T 0 dτ T 0 dτ ′ e −i(ω d ±ω)(τ −τ ′ ) = 4 sin 2 [(ω d ± ω)T /2] (ω d ± ω) 2 ∼ 2π T δ(ω d ± ω),P ω d (T ) = ρ 0 (x d ) 2 ωmax 0 dω 4 sin 2 [(ω d − ω)T /2] (ω d − ω) 2 |β (1) ω | 2 + |β (2) ω | 2 |χ u,out ω (x)| 2 .
